рдЕрде рдкрдЮреНрдЪрдорд╛рдзреНрдпрд╛рдп рдЖрд░рднреНрдпрддреЗ ред рдкреНрд░рдердореЗ рдордгреНрдбрд▓реЗ рдПрдХрд╛рджрд╢реЗрд╜рдиреБрд╡рд╛рдХреЗ рдЪрддреНрд╡рд╛рд░рд┐ рд╕реВрдХреНрддрд╛рдирд┐ рдЧрддрд╛рдирд┐ ред ’ рдкреНрд░ рдордиреНрдорд╣реЗ’ рдЗрддреНрдпреЗрддрддреН рддреНрд░рдпреЛрджрд╢рд░реНрдЪрдВ рдкрдЮреНрдЪрдордВ рд╕реВрдХреНрддрдореН ред рдЕрддреНрд░рд╛рдиреБрдХреНрд░рдореНрдпрддреЗ- рдкреНрд░ рд╕рдкреНрддреЛрдиреЗрддрд┐ ред рдЕрдирд┐рд░реБрдХреНрддрд╛ рд╕рдВрдЦреНрдпрд╛ рд╡рд┐рдВрд╢рддрд┐рдГ ред ( рдЕрдиреБ. резреи. рек ) рдЗрддреНрдпреБрдХреНрддрддреНрд╡рд╛рддреН рдкреНрд░ рд╕рдкреНрддреЛрдиреЗрддреНрдпреБрдХреНрддреЗ рддреНрд░рдпреЛрджрд╢реЗрддреНрдпреБрдХреНрддрдВ рднрд╡рддрд┐ ред тАШ рдЛрд╖рд┐рд╢реНрдЪрд╛рдиреНрдпрд╕реНрдорд╛рддреН ’ рдЗрддрд┐ рдкрд░рд┐рднрд╛рд╖рдпрд╛ рдиреЛрдзрд╛ рдЛрд╖рд┐рдГ ред рдЕрдирд╛рджреЗрд╢рдкрд░рд┐рднрд╛рд╖рдпрд╛ рддреНрд░рд┐рд╖реНрдЯреБрдкреН рдЫрдиреНрджрдГ ред рдЗрдиреНрджреНрд░реЛ рджреЗрд╡рддрд╛ ред рдЧрддрдГ рд╕рд╛рдорд╛рдиреНрдп рд╡рд┐рдирд┐рдпреЛрдЧрдГ ред рд╡рд┐рд╢реЗрд╖рд╡рд┐рдирд┐рдпреЛрдЧрд╕реНрддреБ рд▓рд┐рдЩреНрдЧрд╛рджрд╡рдЧрдиреНрддрд╡реНрдпрдГ рее

+Jamison Brereton

62
Indra
Nodhas Gautama
13 verses: triс╣гс╣нubh
The poetic ingenuity of Nodhas is also on display in this hymn, though in some what different ways from his tricks in I.61. The hymn is full of phonetic play and alliteration, with numerous somewhat aberrant morphological forms generated for this play. Repetition and variation on this repetition are also conspicuousтАФfor example, the word san─Б╠Бt (тАЬfrom of oldтАЭ) opening 8a, 10a, 12a, with teasingly close forms (s├бnemi 9a, s├бn─лс╕Н─Б 10a) alternating with it.
The hymn falls into two main sections. The first five verses are the more conven tionalтАФif poetically richтАФpart, treating the Vala myth and especially the role of the Aс╣Еgirases in it. (Interestingly, the Vala myth is absent from I.61.) The first half of verse 6 seems to summarize this deed of IndraтАЩs extravagantly: тАЬThis is his most conspicuous deed, the dearest wonder of the wondrous one.тАЭ But Nodhas tricks us here, for the second half of the verse concerns an entirely different and much more obscure deed of IndraтАЩs, the swelling of the four, probably celestial, rivers. From 6cd through verse 10 we meet fleetingly recounted fragments of other myths, but these myths seem connected at a structural level, since they involve female figures in pairs or groups working reciprocally or jointly: the rivers of 6cd, the two world-halves of 7 (compared to twin consorts), Night and Dawn (compared to young girls) in 8, black and red cows in 9, and streams again in 10, apparently returning to the rivers of 6cd. In the second half of 10 we encounter a surfeit of females: two words for wives (j├бni and p├бtn─л) as well as sisters, all offering friendship to Indra. These females may refer to the streams of the first half of the verse, or to the thoughts converging on Indra in the next verse (11), which are also compared to wives. Most likely the reference in

10cd is double, providing a transition from the cosmic mythological realm in verses 6тАУ10 to the ritual here-and-now of verses 11тАУ13. The hymn ends, like I.61, with a verse (13) specifically applicable to the тАЬFallow-bay-yokingтАЭ oblation, and like I.61 (vss. 14 and 16) mentions both the poet Nodhas and his clan, the Gotamas.

+Jamison Brereton Notes

Indra Shares much vocabulary with I.61 and sometimes plays off the turns of phrase in that hymn.

+Wilson
English translation:

тАЬWe mediate like Aс╣Еgiras, an acceptable adress to that powerful and praise-deserving Indra, who is to be adored by his worshippers (with prayers) of efficacy, to bring him to the ceremony; let us repeat a prayer to the celebrated leader of all.тАЭ

+Jamison Brereton

We think up a fortifying song for the forceful one who longs for hymns, just as the Aс╣Еgirases did.
A chant with the good twists of a praiser we chant to the one worthy of chant, to the widely famed superior man.

+Jamison Brereton Notes

Rich with phonetic figures: a ┼Ыavas─Бn─Б╠Бya ┼Ы┼лс╣г├бm; b ─Бс╣Еg┼лс╣г├бс╣Г g├нrvaс╣Зase aс╣Еgirasv├бt [note that ─Бс╣Еg┼лс╣г├бm participates in both: it rhymes with preceding ┼Ы┼лс╣г├бm, but its initial matches aс╣Еgirasv├бt, while g├нrvaс╣Зase takes up the 2nd syllable of aс╣Еgirasv├бt]; cd с╣Ыgmiy─Б╠Бya, ├бrc─Бma ark├бm [this last also a clear etymological figure].

┼Ыavas─Бn─Б╠Бya ┼Ы┼лс╣г├бm recalls ┼Ы├бvas─Б ┼Ыuс╣г├бntam of 61.10a in the same metrical position, with our phrase referring to Indra, the one in I.61 to Vс╣Ыtra.

The Pp. reads stuvat├й in c, a reading that is universally followed. But this dative is awkward: if it is coreferential with the other datives in the verse, it must refer to Indra, and Indra тАЬpraisingтАЭ is an odd concept in a hymn devoted instead to presenting praises to him. Although Indra occasionally joins in the verbal parts of the sacrifice (see above ad I.55.4), he does not ordinarily (or ever?) praise others. Nonetheless, this is RenouтАЩs solution: тАЬ(dieu) louangeur.тАЭ Schmidt (1968, Bс╣Ыhaspati und Indra, 163) suggests a variant of this: тАЬden mit guten Liedern (selbst) preisenden,тАЭ but self-praise suggests a medial form, not the active we have. If the participle does not modify Indra, another person in the dative needs to be introduced, despite the unlikelihood of a separate dative referent. This is GeldnerтАЩs solution: тАЬihn [=Indra], der f├╝r den S├дnger тАж zu preisen ist,тАЭ construing stuvat├й with с╣Ыgmiy─Б╠Бya. So also Witzel Got┼Н with slightly different tr., though both they and Geldner consider the тАЬpraisingтАЭ possibility. But the difficulty disappears if, against the Pp., we read instead stuvat├бс╕е, genitive sg. of the participle, as I do.

+Griffith

LIKE Angiras a gladdening laud we ponder to him who loveth song, exceeding mighty.
Let us sing glory to the far-famed Hero who must be praised with fair hymns by the singer.

+Geldner

Wir ersinnen f├╝r den M├дchtigen eine St├дrkung, f├╝r den Lobbegehrenden ein Loblied in der Weise der Angiras. Auf ihn, der f├╝r den S├дnger in sch├╢nen Preisreden zu preisen ist, wollen wir einen Lobgesang singen, auf den ber├╝hmten Herrn.

+Grassmann

Ein Preislied weihn wir andachtsvoll dem starken, dem Liederfreund wie Angiras ein Loblied; Lasst Sang uns singen dem ber├╝hmten Manne, mit Hymnen ihm, der Lob verdient und Ehre.

+Elizarenkova

+Wilson
English translation:

тАЬDo you, priests, offer to the vast and most powerful, Indra, earnest veneration, a chant fit to be sung aloud; for through him our forefathers, the Aс╣Еgirasas, worshipping him, and knowing the footmark, recovered (the stolen) cattle.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

─Ас╣Еguс╣гyam s─Бma = a s─Бma fit to be recited aloud (agho┼Ыyayogyam) such as the rathantaras. Here s─Бma is explained as: с╣Ыkс╣гu yad g─Бnam gasya s─Бmety─Бkhya, singing or changint the с╣Ыc─Бs

+Jamison Brereton

Present your great reverence to the great one, a songful melody to the forceful one,
with which our forefathers, the Aс╣Еgirases, knowing the track [/word], chanting, found the cows.

+Jamison Brereton Notes

This verse is in some ways a double of vs. 1. They both begin with pr├б, and the opening phrases pr├б manmahe and pr├б vo mah├й rhyme, although the two maheтАЩs have entirely different analyses. (Note also the mah├й m├бhi play in 2a.) The next phrase of 1a, ┼Ыavas─Бn─Б╠Бya ┼Ы┼лс╣г├бm, is paralleled in 2b, with the first word identical and s─Б╠Бma substituting for the second (both ┼Ы┼лс╣г├бm and s─Б╠Бma referrring to the verbal product offered to Indra). At the beginning of the second p─Бdas, ─Бс╣Еg┼лс╣гy├аm of 2b matches ─Бс╣Еg┼лс╣г├бm of 1a. тАЬLike the Aс╣ЕgirasesтАЭ (aс╣Еgirasv├бt) of 1b is picked up by the Aс╣Еgirases themselves ├бс╣Еgirasaс╕е in 2d, and the heavy etymology figure involving the root тИЪarc тАШchantтАЩ is reprised by the participle ├бrcantaс╕е in 2d, which opens its p─Бda just as the finite verb ├бrc─Бma does in 1d.

+Griffith

Unto the great bring ye great adoration, a chant with praise to him exceeding mighty,
Through whom our sires, Angirases, singing praises and knowing well the places, found the cattle.

Bringet dem Gro├Яen eure gro├Яe Huldigung dar, die liedgem├д├Яe Sangesweise dem M├дchtigen, mit der unsere Vorv├дter, die spurenkundigen Angiras, unter Lobsingen die Rinder auffanden!

+Grassmann

Dem grossen traget euer gross Gebet vor, laut t├╢nenden Gesang dem kraftbegabten, Durch den des Weges kundig unsre Ahnen, die Angiras, durch Lied die K├╝he fanden.

+Wilson
English translation:

тАЬWhen the search was set on foot by Indra and the Aс╣Еgirasas, Suram─Б secured food for her young; then Bс╣Ыhaspati slew the devourer and rescued the kine, and the gods, with the cattle, proclaimed their joy alouс╕НтАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Saram─Б agreed with Indra to go in search of the stolen cattle on condition that the milk of the cows should be given to her young ones. Bс╣Ыhaspati = Indra, the master of the great ones (bс╣Ыhatam), the gods

+Jamison Brereton

At the desire of Indra and the Aс╣Еgirases, Saram─Б found the wellspring for posterity.
Br╠еhaspati: he split the rock; he found the cows. The superior men
bellowed together with the ruddy (cows).

+Jamison Brereton Notes

I follow Janert (1956, Sinne und Bedeutung des Wortes тАЬdh─БsiтАЭ und seiner Belegstellen im Rigveda und Awesta) in taking dh─Бs├н- as тАШwellspringтАЩ.

The post-caesura phrase in c bhin├бd ├бdriс╣Г vid├бd g─Б╠Бс╕е contains rhyming verbs followed by their objects; the disyllable ├бdrim contrasts with monosyllable g─Б╠Бс╕е, creating an almost syncopated effect. The strict parallelism of the two VPs may account for the unnecessary accent on bhin├бt, which functions rhetorically like a fronted verb, just like vid├бt. In fact, as my tr. indicates, I consider the initial subject bс╣Ы╠Бhasp├бtiс╕е to be essentially extrasentential, a very topicalized topic.

The second of the VPs is also a mirror-image of the final phrase in 2d g─Б╠Б ├бvindan (imperfect in 2, versus aorist in 3), and it can be considered a тАЬrepairтАЭ of the somewhat opaque vid├бt тАж dh─Бs├нm тАЬfound the wellspringтАЭ that intervenes (3b).

+Wilson
English translation:

тАЬPowerful Indra, who are to be gratified with a laudatory and well-accented hymn by the seven priests, whether engaged for nine months, or for ten; and, desirous of (safe) protection, you have terrified by your voice the divisible fructifying cloud.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Vipras (priests) are aс╣Еgirasas: (1) those who conduct sacrifices for nine months; and (2) those who conduct the sacrifices for ten months. The seven priests are Medh─Бtithi and other с╣Ыс╣гis of Aс╣Еgiras lineage. valam = cloud;

Adrim = that which is to be divided by the thunderbolt;

Phaligam = that which yields fruit;

Alternative: adri = mountain;

Phaliga = a cloud;

Vala = an asura

+Jamison Brereton

He with (his flock) of good rhythm, he with rhythm, with the seven inspired poets, with the Navagvas, he, the resounding one, with sound (split) the rock.
With the hastening ones, o able Indra, you cleft the bolt, with a roar you cleft Vala with the Da┼Ыagvas.

+Jamison Brereton Notes

The repetitions and lexical and phonological echoes that have served Nodhas well in the first three verses increase here, to an almost incantatory level. To point to a few of these: a s├б suс╣гс╣н├║bh─Б s├б stubh─Б╠Б(again a syncopation, as in 3c); b svar├йс╣Зa тАж svar├н yo тАж; c: saraс╣Зyubhiс╕е echoes the s├бтАЩs of a and the s(v)arтАЩs of b, while finding its own faint echo in the final ┼Ыakra of the p─Бda; d r├бveс╣Зa is a virtual anagram of svar├йс╣Зa in b, darayo is almost a rhyme form of svar├н yo in b in the same metrical position, and both svar├н yo and darayo precede the hemistich-final words ending in - gvaiс╕е.

All other tr. take this verse as a single sentence with 2nd ps. subj. However, as I have shown (1992: тАЬsa fig├йтАЭ), s├б with 2nd ps. reference is limited to imperatives and should not appear with an injunctive like darayaс╕е. I therefore consider the first hemistich to continue the 3rd ps. reference of vs. 3, with a switch to 2nd ps. in the second half. This type of switch is quite common in the RV.

In c I do not take saraс╣Зy├║- and phalig├б- as personal names, pace Geldner et al. The tr. тАШboltтАЩ for the latter follows Hiersche (Asp., тАШRiegel, VershlussтАЩ), based on a possible connection with parigha- (Up+) тАШiron bar for shutting a door/gateтАЩ; see EWA s.v. However, as Elizabeth Tucker points out, a less specific sense such as тАШbarrierтАЩ would fit the contexts better and would also make fewer assumptions about early technology.

+Griffith

Mid shout, loud shout, and roar, with the Navagvas, seven singers, hast thou, heavenly, rent the mountain;
Thou hast, with speeders, with Dasagvas, Indra, Sakra, with thunder rent obstructive Vala.

+Geldner

Du sprengtest mit der schmetternden Schar, du unter schmetterndem Tone mit den sieben Redegewaltigen, laut mit leiser Stimme zusammen mit den Navagva┬┤s den Fels, mit den Saranyu┬┤s den Phaliga, o m├дchtiger Indra, unter Geschrei den Vala im Bunde mit den Dasagva┬┤s.

+Grassmann

Mit Jubelruf mit lautem Schall zerbrachst du mit sieben S├дngern, mit der Schar der Neuner Den Fels mit Krachen, o gewalt’ger Indra, die Wolkenh├╢hle mit den raschen Zehnern.

+Wilson
English translation:

тАЬDestroyer of foes, praised by the Aс╣Еgirasas, you have scattered the darkness with the dawn, and with the rays of the sun; you have made straight the elevations of the earth; you have strengthened the foundations of the ethereal region.тАЭ

+Jamison Brereton

Being hymned by the Aс╣Еgirases, o wondrous one, you uncovered the blinding darkness [/soma stalk] along with the dawn, with the sun,
with the cows.
You spread out the back of the earth, Indra; you propped up the nearer realm of heaven.

+Jamison Brereton Notes

The instrumentals in b (uс╣г├бs─Б s┼л╠Бryeс╣Зa g├│bhiс╕е) are not parallel with ├бс╣Еgirobhiс╕е in a. The Aс╣Еgirases there are the agents of the passively used participle gс╣Ыс╣З─Бn├бс╕е (and also potentially instrumental of accompaniment with the main verb, as IndraтАЩs helpers: тАЬalong with the AтАЩs you uncoveredтАжтАЭ). The instrumentals in b are the additional elements that Indra uncovered.

├бndhaс╕е is a potential pun, as a homophone meaning both тАШblind darknessтАЩ and тАШsoma stalkтАЩ. The former is surely the first reading in this treatment of the Vala myth, with the cave a black hole, as it were. So Geldner, Renou, Witzel Got┼Н. But soma is never far from IndraтАЩs mind, and the cosmogonic deeds with which he is credited in the second hemistich are often performed under the influence of soma. Schmidt (p. 164) favors тАШ(Soma-)FlutтАЩ, following Bergaigne and L├╝ders.

Praised by Angirases, thou, foe-destroyer, hast, with the Dawn, Sun, rays, dispelled the darkness.
Thou Indra, hast spread out the earth’s high ridges, and firmly fixed the region under heaven.

+Grassmann

Von Angiras ger├╝hmt, enth├╝lltest, hehrer, das Dunkel du durch Morgen, Sonne, Strahlen, Du Indra dehntest aus der Erde R├╝cken, befestigtest den untern Raum des Himmels.

+Wilson
English translation:

тАЬThe deeds of that graceful Indra are most admirable; his exploits are most glorious, in that he has replenished the four rivers of sweet water, spread over the surface of the earth.тАЭ

+Jamison Brereton

This is his most conspicuous deed, the dearest wonder of the
wondrous one,
that he made the four nearer (celestial?) rivers with their honeyed floods swell in their eddying.

+Jamison Brereton Notes

This verse has to be NodhasтАЩs joke. It is conspicuously placed, as the central verse of the hymn and thus a potential omphalos, and it announces IndraтАЩs deed (k├бrma) with extraordinary fanfare, including two superlatives (pr├бyakс╣гatamam тАж c─Б╠Бrutamam). But in a hymn so far devoted to what is one of IndraтАЩs greatest deeds, the opening of the Vala cave, the poet springs on us instead a deed of utter obscurity involving the swelling of four rivers, an act with no other clear mentions in the RV.

Geldner valiantly seeks parallels, but the two passages he adduces (I.104.3-4 and I.174.7) have little or nothing in common with our passage and itтАЩs not even clear that there are rivers in the second. L├╝ders (Varuс╣Зa 335-37) predictably sees these as heavenly rivers тАФ four because they flow тАЬnach den vier Himmelsgegenden.тАЭ Renou also sees them as associated with heaven, while Schmidt (Bс╣Ыhaspati und Indra 164) and Witzel Got┼Н seem to see them as being тАЬunderтАЭ (something unspecified). I have no solution for the affinities of this scrap of mythology, although I tend to agree generally with L├╝ders that the rivers are more likely to be celestial than terrestrial, and I would suggest that it may have to do with producing rain. But I still think the point here is that Nodhas has set us up for a grand announcement and then, by bait and switch, given us a myth that none of us has ever heard of.

The asti in this hemistich may provide another bit of evidence for this view.

Unaccented asti almost always has existential value (тАЬthere existsтАжтАЭ) because the 3rd sg. pres. copula in equational sentences (тАЬX is YтАЭ) is regularly (indeed, probably by rule) gapped. But an existential sense here doesnтАЩt work (тАЬThere exists this most conspicuous deedтАжтАЭ). I suggest that the overt asti here signals a strong assertion in the face of expected opposition (тАЬThis is his most conspicuous deedтАЭ - rather like American childrenтАЩs quarrels: тАЬis tooтАЭ тАЬis notтАЭ). The disputed word upahvar├й adds to the obscurity of this bit of myth. It is clearly a derivative of тИЪhvс╣Ы тАШgo crookedly, deviateтАЩ, but the exact sense of this nominal derivative is not clear. In this passage Geldner takes it as тАШlapтАЩ (Schoss), Renou as тАШfold, creaseтАЩ (repli), Witzel Got┼Н as тАШabyss, chasmтАЩ (Abgrund), while Schmidt bypasses any literal rendering with тАЬim Felsen.тАЭ L├╝ders considers it the name тАЬf├╝r den Beh├дlter des himmlischen Urquells,тАЭ which of course fits his larger picture of the heavenly ocean. I consider the word to convey in the first instance a visual image, that of a meander or deviation from the straight. In connection with rivers (as also in VIII.96.14) it refers to eddies, the circular or oval shapes produced against the current by uneven flow – or to the тАЬoxbowsтАЭ or тАЬmeandersтАЭ created in a riverтАЩs course by such uneven flow (Google images are quite striking). In other geographical contexts it can refer to byways, detours from the straight path, and ultimately to remote places, the backeddies, as it were, of the mountains.

+Wilson
English translation:

тАЬHe who is not to be attained by violence, but (is easily propitiated) by those who praise him with sacred hymns, parted twofold the eternal and united (sphered of heaven and earth); the graceful Indra cherished the heaven and earth, like the sun in the august and most excellent sky.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Ay─Бsya: ya┼Ыa, effort; hence, ay─Бsya, that which is not attainable by effort (or, not to be overcome in battle)

+Jamison Brereton

Another time, with his chants that were being sung, the irrepressible one uncovered the age-old pair belonging to the same nest [=the two worlds].
As Bhaga [/a (good) portion] (supports) his two consorts, he of very wondrous power supported the two world-halves in farthest heaven.

+Jamison Brereton Notes

Nodhas continues to deploy his tricks in this verse. He has moved on from the obscure four-river reference in the preceding verse, to a more standard domain for Indra to display his power: the two world halves. But in the first hemistich Indra is not depicted as filling them or propping them apart or any of his usual actions with regard to them, but rather as тАЬuncoveringтАЭ them, using the same lexeme v├н тИЪvс╣Ы as was just used in a Vala-myth verse (5a v├н var, 7a v├н vavre). (That lexeme is close to being the signature Vala verb, though it is actually more commonly ├бpa тИЪvс╣Ы, with the same sense, but a different preverb.) Notice that the two occurrences of v├н тИЪvс╣Ы surround the pseudo-omphalos verse 6, in the usual manner in which concentric lexical rings signal an omphalos. So Nodhas thus presents this new mythological theme as if it were the old one, the Vala myth found in vss. 2-5, though he is entering different mythological territory.

He also reuses the тИЪс╣Ыc lexical theme from vss. 1-2, with arka├нс╕е in b, which is apparently qualified by the middle participle st├бvam─Бnebhiс╕е (remember stuvat├б in 1c). This participle is somewhat disturbing; like all middle participles to the root тИЪstu, it is used passively here, but unlike all the other passive uses, its subject is not the god praised but the praises themselves. It may be that Nodhas want his audience to take notice of such an aberrant usage. However, I am tempted to read

*stavam─Бnaibh├нс╕е here - that is, nom. sg. st├бvam─Бnas + pron. ebh├нс╕е, with rare double application of sandhi: -as + e- > -a + e- > -ai-. The participle st├бvam─Бnas would modify Indra, as would be expected; ebh├нс╕е occurs several times with arka├нс╕е (IV.3.15, 10.13) and the passive of тИЪstu also appears with this instr. (see passages adduced by Geldner in n. 7b, where he suggests a similar, but far from identical, reanalysis of the form in the text, calling it тАЬvielleicht HypallageтАЭ). If the emendation is accepted, I would change the tr. to тАЬthe irrepressible one being praised by these chants.тАЭ The arka├нс╕е might also be construed secondarily as it currently is in the tr., with v├н vavre, as the instrument with which he performed the uncovering.

ay─Б╠Бsya- regularly qualifies Indra; there is no reason to take it as a personal name, identifying a distinct second figure here (pace Geldner, Renou). Phonetic figure: san├бj─Б s├бn─лс╕╖e taking up the saтАЩs in vs. 4 and prefiguring the saтАЩs that will be conspicuous in the next few verses.

The second hemistich contains a clever pun between simile and frame. In the frame (p─Бda d) Indra supported, that is, held up, the two world halves in familiar fashion (somewhat repairing the less standard uncovering he performed in ab, though cf. VIII.96.16). In this task he is compared to the god Bhaga (though it is not an activity that I think of as particularly associated with Bhaga). But in the simile bh├бga- is used as a common noun тАШ(good) portionтАЩ, which provides support for two consorts (m├йne) - the point presumably being that a man needs a particularly large portion of property to support two wives. I do not follow HoffmannтАЩs view (1960: 245; KZ 76) that m├йne here is an elliptical dual referring to concubine and lawful wife, and in fact believe that m├йn─Б in general has a wider sense than he allows. He restricts it to concubines, but the derivation from тИЪmi тАШexchangeтАЩ suggests a wider application, with the wife as an тАЬexchange tokenтАЭ in the economic transaction of marriage.

+Griffith

Unwearied, won with lauding hymns, he parted of old the ancient Pair, united ever.
In highest sky like Bhaga, he the doer of marvels set both Dames and earth and heaven.

+Wilson
English translation:

тАЬ(Night and dawn), of various complexion, repeatedly born, but ever youthful, have traversed in their revolutions alternately, from a remote period, earth and heaven; night with her dark, dawn with her luminous limbs.тАЭ

+Jamison Brereton

From of old the two young girls of distinct forms, (ever) regenerating, (go) around heaven and earth along their own coursesтАФ
Night with her black, Dawn with her gleaming white shapes progress one after the other.

+Jamison Brereton Notes

From the spatial dual females of vs. 7 Nodhas now moves to the temporal: night and dawn, also dual females, whose activities have kept occurring тАЬfrom of oldтАЭ (san─Б╠Бt), linking them lexically to the two world-halves of 7a who were born of old (san├бj─Б).

In d├нvam p├бri bh┼л╠Бm─Б we encounter the not-quite-dvandva also met in 61.14; there in dy─Б╠Бv─Б ca bh┼л╠Бm─Б, dy─Б╠Бv─Б was definitely dual, but its partner bh┼л╠Бm─Б was either singular (with lengthened final) or plural. Here d├нvam is definitely singular, with bh┼л╠Бm─Б either of the choices just given, probably singular.

punarbh┼л╠Б- later (already in the AV) has a specialized legal application as a designation for a remarried widow (see Thieme Kl.Sch. 445-60 = KZ 78, 1963), but this sense does not seem to be in play here or in the other RV passages.

Though most take akt─Б╠Б(which only appears here) as the ppl. of тИЪa├▒j тАШanointтАЩ, serving as a poetic designation for тАШnightтАЩ, I favor a root connection with the тАШnightтАЩ word, with zero-grade in the root syllable, esp. since akt├║- тАШnightтАЩ is probably so connected (pace EWA s.v.). See EWA s.v. and AiG III.234, though both are skeptical. Of course secondary association with тИЪa├▒j would be natural.

Jesse Lundquist notes the chiasmic kс╣Ыс╣гс╣З├йbhir akt─Б╠Б-uс╣г─Б╠Бr├║┼Ыadbhiс╕е of c; for a similar chiasmus see 11c.

Joshua T. Katz suggests that v├бpus- here and in other dawn/night passages refers to bridal clothing and suggests a tr. тАШfineryтАЩ.

+Griffith

Still born afresh, young Dames, each in her manner, unlike in hue, the Pair in alternation
Round heaven and earth from ancient time have travelled, Night with her dark limbs, Dawn with limbs of splendour.

+Geldner

Seit alters umwandeln die beiden ungleichen Jungfrauen aus eigenem Triebe Himmel und Erde, immer wiederkehrend: die Nacht mit dunklen Farben angetan, die Morgenr├╢te mit hellen, kommen sie eine um die andere.

+Grassmann

Stets wandeln um den Himmel, um die Erde, an Farbe ungleich, neu sich stets verj├╝ngend, Nach ihrer Art mit schwarzem, rothem Schmucke die Nacht und Morgenr├╢the nacheinander.

+Elizarenkova

+Wilson
English translation:

тАЬThe son of strength assiduous in good works, diligent in pious acts, retains his ancient friendship (for his votary). You, (Indra), provide within the yet immature cows, whether black or red, the mature and glossy milk.тАЭ

+Jamison Brereton

Possessing very wondrous power, doing good work, the son with his strength supported the partnership to its full extent.
You placed the тАЬcookedтАЭ (milk) even within the тАЬrawтАЭ (cows), the
gleaming white milk in the black and in the red (cows).

+Jamison Brereton Notes

Although the first hemistich is perfectly easy to tr., it is not at all clear what it is referring to. First, what is the тАЬpartnershipтАЭ (sakhy├бm)? Geldner thinks it is with the beings named in vss. 7-11, while S─Бyaс╣Зa thinks rather of the sacrificers. Nothing in the context helps to define what type of partnership it is or with whom, and the verb that governs it, тАЬsupportedтАЭ (d─Бdh─Бra), seems oddly chosen. The subject of this verb is also not fully identified: the larger context of course points to Indra, and sud├бс╣Гs─Бс╕е тАШpossessing very wondrous powerтАЩ was just used in 7c of Indra (where he was likewise the subject of a form of тИЪdhс╣Ы), and see d├бс╣Гsaс╕е applied to his deed in 6b.

But who is he the тАЬsonтАЭ (s┼лn├║-) of? and more to the point, the epithet ┼Ы├бvasaс╕е s┼лn├║с╕е тАЬson of strengthтАЭ is ordinarily AgniтАЩs. Here we have the variant s┼лn├║с╕е тАж ┼Ы├бvas─Б with instr., and s┼лn├║с╕е ┼Ы├бvas─Б is used of Agni in I.27.2. On the other hand, ┼Ыavas─Бn├бis twice use of Indra in the beginning of this hymn (1a, 2b) and ┼Ыavas─Бvan in 11d, ┼Ыavas─Бna in 13c; cf. also the clear ┼Ы├бvasaс╕е s┼лn├║m ├нndram in IV.24.1. I tentatively suggest that there is an attempt to blur the lines between Indra and Agni here, and that the partnership or fellowship may be the mutually beneficial relationship between gods and men centered on the sacrifice, where Agni officiates. But I cannot point to hard evidence for this interpretation.

The paradox of the cooked milk in the raw cows is a standard one, often trotted out as a natural wonder to contemplate. Here it seems to be taken further: there are literal milk and literal cows in c, but in d the black and red cows are likely to be the nights and dawns (as in 8cd) and the тАЬgleaming white milkтАЭ the early light known as the тАЬmilk of the dawn cowsтАЭ (see Watkins MoreDawnCows). As far as I can see, this hemistich has no integral connection with the first half of the verse, and the shift from 3rd to 2nd person underlines this disconnect.

+Griffith

Rich in good actions, skilled in operation, the Son with might maintains his perfect friendship.
Thou in the raw cows, black of hue or ruddy, storest the ripe milk glossy white in colour.

+Geldner

+Grassmann

Sch├╢nwirkend reich an Wunderkraft bewahrte der Sohn mit Kraft die unversehrte Freundschaft; In rohe K├╝he legtest du die gare, die weisse Milch in schwarze und in rothe.

+Elizarenkova

+Wilson
English translation:

тАЬFrom a remote time the contiguous, unshifting, and unwearied fingers practise with (all) their energies many thousand acts of devotion (towards Indra); and like the wives (of the gods), the protecting sisters worship him who is without shame.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Pan─лс╕е svas─Бraс╕е = the protecting or propitiatory and moving epithets of avaniс╕е, fingers; janayo na = like wives (i.e., of the gods, dev─Бn─Бm)

+Jamison Brereton

From of old the streams coming from the same nest, unquenchable and immortal, guard their commandments with their powers.
For many thousands (of aeons?), like lady wives, the sisters [=streams? thoughts?] offer friendship to the unabashed one.

+Jamison Brereton Notes

A nicely balanced alliterative p─Бda san─Б╠Бt s├бn─лс╕╖─Б av├бn─лr av─Бt─Б╠Б, with the first phrase picking up 7a san├бj─Б s├бn─лс╕╖e. Note also that the first word of b, vrat─Б╠Б, echoes av─Бt─Б╠Б. On av─Бt├б- see comm. ad I.38.7 and VIII.79.7.

Again the reference of this verse is somewhat obscure. Are the streams here the same as the four rivers in 6cd? or are they the streams released from Vс╣ЫtraтАЩs confinement? (Geldner suggests sensibly that Indra gave them the command to wander in I.61.12, though it is their own commandments, not IndraтАЩs, that they safeguard here.) The identity of the sisters of cd, compared to wives, is even less clear. Are they the same streams as ab? or possibly praise-songs (g├нraс╕е appear with the same simile, j├бnayo n├б p├бtn─лс╕е in I.186.7, and in our next vs., 11cd, тАЬinspired thoughtsтАЭ [man─лс╣г─Б╠Бс╕е] are compared to eager wives). Again, I think the reference is supposed to be shifting, with the literal streams of mythological time replaced by the thoughts and praises of the ritual here-and-now, mediated by the feminine gender of the words used of both streams and praises. The temporal shift in this part of the hymn, or rather the double temporal vision, is signalled by the repeated initial san─Б╠Бt тАШfrom of oldтАЩ and related terms (8a, 10a, 11a, 12a, 13a) and its contrast with the present tense verbs that prevail in this section.

+Griffith

Their paths, of old connected, rest uninjured; they with great might preserve the immortal statutes.
For many thousand holy works the Sisters wait on the haughty Lord like wives and matrons.

+Geldner

Seit alters halten die verschwisterten Str├╢me, die unsterblichen, die durch keine Gewalten bezwungen werden, seine Gebote. Viele tausend Schwestern beeifern sich um ihn wie verm├дhlte Frauen um den nicht Sch├╝chternen.

+Grassmann

Die G├╢tter wahren stets die heil’ge Ordnung, mit Kraft die ew’gen Str├╢me, die vereinten; Die vielen tausend Schwestern, sie verehren den k├╝hnen Helden wie verm├дhlte Frauen.

+Elizarenkova

+Wilson
English translation:

тАЬBeautiful Indra, who are to be praised with holy hymns, the pious who are desirous of holy rites, those who are anxious for riches, and those who are wise, repair to you with veneration; powerful Indra, their minds adhere to you, as affectionate wives to a loving husbaс╣Зс╕Н+тАЭ

+Jamison Brereton

The age-old thoughts, with reverence, with chants, seeking goods, have run anew (to you), wondrous one.
Like eager wives their eager husband, the inspired thoughts caress you, you strong one.

+Jamison Brereton Notes

san─Бy├║- тАШage-oldтАЩ is found only here, and was clearly created to match the other p─Бda-initial san─Б- тАШoldтАЩ forms in this section, while matching its nom. pl.

san─Бy├║vo with vas┼лy├бvo, which opens the next p─Бda. Jesse Lundquist suggests that part of the motivation for creating this particular hapax is to invoke the paradoxical juxtaposition of sana- тАШoldтАЩ and yuvan- тАШyouthтАЩ. Verse 8 contains the same juxtaposition: san─Б╠Бt тАж yuvat─л╠БтАЬfrom of old the two young girls,тАЭ and the theme of old and new runs throughout this hymn, indeed in this verse.

Jesse Lundquist also notes the chiasmic p├бtiс╣Г n├б p├бtn─лr u┼Ыat─л╠Бr u┼Ы├бntam of c.

+Griffith

Thoughts ancient, seeking wealth, with adoration, with newest lauds have sped to thee, O Mighty.
As yearning wives cleave to their yearning husband, so cleave our hymns to thee, O Lord most potent.

+Geldner

Ehrf├╝rchtig sind die altgewohnten frommen Gedanken aufs neue mit Preisreden, um Gutes bittend, zu dir geeilt, du Meister. Die Gedicht h├дtscheln dich wie verliebte Gattinnen den Gatten, du Machtvoller.

+Grassmann

Die alten Lieder eilen neu, o hehrer, zu dir voll Andacht preisend, Gut begehrend, Wie voll Begier die Frauen zu dem Gatten, liebkosend dich, o starker, die Ges├дnge.

+Wilson
English translation:

тАЬBeautiful Indra, the riches that have long since been held in your hands have suffered neither loss nor diminution. You, Indra, are illustrious, addicted to good works, and resolute; enrich us, you who are diligent in action, by your acts.тАЭ

+Jamison Brereton

Though (originating) from of old, the riches in your hand do not become exhausted, nor do they give out, wondrous one.
You are brilliant, Indra, you are resolute and insightful. Do your best for us with your abilities, able one.

+Griffith

Strong God, the riches which thy hands have holden from days of old have perished not nor wasted.
Splendid art thou, O Indra, wise, unbending: strengthen us with might, O Lord of Power.

+Geldner

Seit alters her schon nehmen die Reicht├╝mer in deiner Hand nicht ab, noch versiegen sie, du Meister. Du bist glanzvoll, einsichtsvoll, weise, o Indra. Bem├╝h dich f├╝r uns nach deinen Kr├дften, du Kraftvoller!

+Grassmann

In deinem Arme dauern stets die G├╝ter, o hehrer, nie verk├╝mmern noch vergehn sie; Du, Indra, bist gewaltig, glanzreich, weise; o st├дrke, starker, uns mit deiner St├дrke.

+Wilson
English translation:

тАЬMighty Indra, Nodhas, the son of Gotama, has composed for us this new hymn, (addressed) to you, who have been forever, who harness your coursers (to your car) and are the sure guide (of all). May he who has acquired wealth by propitious acts, come hither quickly in the morning.тАЭ

+Jamison Brereton

For the age-old тАЬFallow-bay-yokingтАЭ (oblation) Gotama has fashioned a newer sacred formulation, IndraтАФ
Nodhas (has fashioned it) for us for good guidance, o forceful one. тАУ Early in the morningтАФsoonтАФhe should come, bringing goods
through his insight.

+Jamison Brereton Notes

san─Бy├бnt- is likewise only here. Note its semantic opposite n├бvyam at the end of the p─Бda, the same contrast as san─Бy├║vo тАж n├бvyo in 11a.

Like the last verse of the preceding hymn (I.61.16), this verse is extra-hymnic in some ways, but also has connections with the rest of the hymn. The тАЬoldтАЭ pattern continues to open the verse, and the voc. ┼Ыavas─Бna in c forms a ring with the same stem in the dative in 1a.

+Griffith

O mighty Indra, Gotama’s son Nodhas hath fashioned this new prayer to thee Eternal,
Sure leader, yoker of the Tawny Coursers. May he, enriched with prayer, come soon and early.

+Geldner

Ein neues Erbauungslied hat dir zu der altgewohnten Falbenschirrung Nodhas, der Gotamide, verfertigt, o Indra, der du unser guter F├╝hrer bist, du Gewaltiger. - Recht bald am Morgen soll sich der einstellen, der durch die Weisheit Sch├дtze erwirbt.

+Grassmann

Ein neu Gebet hat, Indra, dir, dem alten, dem F├╝chseschirrer, der uns herrlich leitet, O starker, Nodhas Gotama bereitet. Der huldvoll sorget, komme fr├╝h am Morgen.

+Elizarenkova
